  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_cbfac006-7491-47ab-8061-4d521b221669'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5686451771199788311">En las páginas de <i>La Regenta</i> el joven catedrático de la Universidad de Oviedo, Leopoldo Alas, pretendía crear un gran fresco narrativo sobre la sociedad burguesa y urbana, dominada por la nobleza decadente, el clero egoísta y la política caciquil, centrado en la seducción y el adulterio de una dama, Ana Ozores, representativa de aquella sociedad. Influido por la escuela na</span>
  <span id="freeText5686451771199788311" style="display:none">En las páginas de <i>La Regenta</i> el joven catedrático de la Universidad de Oviedo, Leopoldo Alas, pretendía crear un gran fresco narrativo sobre la sociedad burguesa y urbana, dominada por la nobleza decadente, el clero egoísta y la política caciquil, centrado en la seducción y el adulterio de una dama, Ana Ozores, representativa de aquella sociedad. Influido por la escuela naturalista fundamentada en Zola y Maupassant, pero anclado aún en argumentos románticos, Leopoldo Alas "Clarín" escribió la que ha sido considerada como la mejor novela del siglo XIX español. <br /><br />Gregorio Torres Nebrera es catedrático de Literatura Española de la Universidad de Extremadura. Especialista en los siglos XIX y XX, de su trabajo destacan sus estudios y ediciones de Larra, Zorrilla, Valle-Inclán, Azorín, Alberti, y de los autores del teatro español contemporáneo.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5686451771199788311" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17250652895857649572">"Plenty of intrigues, plenty of petty politicking, plenty of material interests", 12 October 2015<br/>	<br/>This review is from: La Regenta (Penguin Classics) (Paperback)<br/>Verified Purchase(What is this?)<br/>This review is from: La Regenta (Classics) (Paperback)<br/>A mammoth read at 700 pages plus, but an absolutely superb one, up with Trollope, Tolstoy and all the greats.<br/>The eponymous Regenta is the judge's wife, a beautiful young woman living in a chaste marriage with an elderly husband. Her confessor, Don Fermin</span>
  <span id="freeText17250652895857649572" style="display:none">"Plenty of intrigues, plenty of petty politicking, plenty of material interests", 12 October 2015<br />	<br />This review is from: La Regenta (Penguin Classics) (Paperback)<br />Verified Purchase(What is this?)<br />This review is from: La Regenta (Classics) (Paperback)<br />A mammoth read at 700 pages plus, but an absolutely superb one, up with Trollope, Tolstoy and all the greats.<br />The eponymous Regenta is the judge's wife, a beautiful young woman living in a chaste marriage with an elderly husband. Her confessor, Don Fermin, encourages her in the excessive religious observance to which she is prone, but is falling in love with her himself... Meanwhile local man-about-town, Alvaro, has vowed to conquer this supposedly virtuous lady, though he is only too aware of his ever-present rival ("I've raised the siege but how do I know that he hasn't mined his way in?")<br />The three main characters have a wonderful supporting cast: the other priests who are jealous of Don Fermino's success, and are plotting his downfall; the young society women of the town; double-dealing servants and the comical members of the gentlemen's club. Not to mention Ana's husband, whose life revolves aroung hunting and the theatre; and Don Fermino's conniving mother, with her lard poultices!<br />This is a book that will draw you in, right from the first chapter, in which Don Fermino has ascended to the church tower where he is surveying the local people with his telescope...<br />Superb translation too; I don't know how this work is so little-known, it's marvellous.</span>

<div id="review_14815293" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14815293" />
    <a title="Joey Warner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/335165-joey-warner"><img alt="Joey Warner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1188915310p2/335165.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14815293?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joey Warner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joey Warner" href="/user/show/335165-joey-warner">Joey Warner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14815293" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9879849924775074106">The edition I (mostly) read was published by Clasicos Castalia, not the one shown on this page.<br/><br/>La Regenta is argueably the best-known, best-written novel of the Spanish 19th century - a masterpiece of Naturalism. This enormously lengthy work tells the story of the little vespiary of Vetusta, Spain and the cruelty of its citizens, particularly toward Ana Ozores, La Regenta (The Regent's Wife). <br/><br/>In short, Ana has married a rich man who spends all his time working on goofy scientific inventions with</span>
  <span id="freeText9879849924775074106" style="display:none">The edition I (mostly) read was published by Clasicos Castalia, not the one shown on this page.<br /><br />La Regenta is argueably the best-known, best-written novel of the Spanish 19th century - a masterpiece of Naturalism. This enormously lengthy work tells the story of the little vespiary of Vetusta, Spain and the cruelty of its citizens, particularly toward Ana Ozores, La Regenta (The Regent's Wife). <br /><br />In short, Ana has married a rich man who spends all his time working on goofy scientific inventions with his friends and has no interest in his wife sexually. Ana, nonetheless, is the most beautiful woman in Vetusta. The local Magistrate, Don Fermin de Pas, and the local don Juan, Alvaro Mesias, try to seduce Ana, heretofore a very chaste woman. The whole town is watching and alive with venomous gossip, and Ana begins to feels the pressure. The floodgates finally give and Alvaro succeeds in seducing Ana, who promptly abandons her. The novel ends with Ana a broken woman, and the reader feels for her. The rest of the town is despicable, by and large, and notably very unhappy.<br /><br />Naturalism, which followed Realism, and is an intensified version of Realism, is a literary movement in which humankind is treated as a kind of godless scientific experiment. The author "winds up", in a manner of speaking, the characters, lets them go like tops, and what ever happens, happens. One major theme of the novel is free will. Another is hypocrisy. Who is to blame for la regenta's fall? She is turned into a social pariah for (as the reader sees it) ultimately giving into pressures, both social and biological. The men who seduce her are in the same boat, in a sense, though at a distinct advantage because of their sex. The townspeople are extremely judgmental, even vicious, and quick to condemn, yet many of them are guilty (one assumes) of the same "sins" they revile.<br /><br />I would have given this novel 5 stars but for the tedium of the description. I have little patience for long, naturalistic works. This one is a great exception, though.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9879849924775074106" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1851088711" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1851088711" />
    <a title="Monty Milne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14797192-monty-milne"><img alt="Monty Milne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363450934p2/14797192.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1851088711?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Monty Milne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Monty Milne" href="/user/show/14797192-monty-milne">Monty Milne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140443462" href="/book/show/765565.La_Regenta">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1851088711" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer464789767635427809">This novel is a very great work of art, but I had to work hard to appreciate it. I'm fine with obscure references to matters classical, biblical or historical, but there are repeated references to minor Spanish poets and playwrights that I had never heard of before (and will doubtless never hear about again). Sometimes these references broadened my understanding (once I'd looked them up in the footnotes), but more often they made me feel dull and slow-witted. The style is subtle and psychologica</span>
  <span id="freeText464789767635427809" style="display:none">This novel is a very great work of art, but I had to work hard to appreciate it. I'm fine with obscure references to matters classical, biblical or historical, but there are repeated references to minor Spanish poets and playwrights that I had never heard of before (and will doubtless never hear about again). Sometimes these references broadened my understanding (once I'd looked them up in the footnotes), but more often they made me feel dull and slow-witted. The style is subtle and psychologically acute, such as the way the text switches from the narrator to the points of view of successive characters and back again...powerfully effective, but also a recipe for confusion unless you are paying close attention. And given that the novel has more than 700 closely written pages, it is also a recipe for ennui, at least in parts.<br /><br />Despite these reservations, it is still a magnificent achievement. It's almost like a darker Spanish Barchester Chronicles - but of course the Catholic Church here is far more corrupt and sinister than the Anglican Church in Trollope. There are also lots of memorable minor characters, and even quite a lot of humour (though you need your irony detector switched on). It is less successful than the Barchester Chronicles in that the structure feels less well integrated and some of the chapters meander into boredom - but that may be my fault, not the author's. Where it scores over Trollope is in the depth of its psychological understanding, and the brilliance of its portrayal of snobbery and provincialism (also far better than anything in Mrs Gaskell). I have never been to Oviedo, but now I have a desire to go there. Wandering into the old town and through the cathedral precincts will be a much more powerful experience, with the memory of this book in my mind.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="464789767635427809" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_298619011" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/298619011" />
    <a title="Bruno Bouchet" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2203561-bruno-bouchet"><img alt="Bruno Bouchet" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442467375p2/2203561.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/298619011?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bruno Bouchet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bruno Bouchet" href="/user/show/2203561-bruno-bouchet">Bruno Bouchet</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2203561-bruno-bouchet?shelf=put-it-back">put-it-back</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer298619011" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6671621071090419777">This was a book that I had wanted to read for years. I love Spain, love 19th Century novels, love the fascinating female central character. If there was ever a book destined for my favourites shelf next to Madame Bovary, this was it. The first couple of paragraphs were wonderfully evocative, I felt transported to the lazy heat of Spain... but then the author started with all the names. At first I couldn’t believe he was introducing so many different characters, I felt like I’d walked round a cor</span>
  <span id="freeText6671621071090419777" style="display:none">This was a book that I had wanted to read for years. I love Spain, love 19th Century novels, love the fascinating female central character. If there was ever a book destined for my favourites shelf next to Madame Bovary, this was it. The first couple of paragraphs were wonderfully evocative, I felt transported to the lazy heat of Spain... but then the author started with all the names. At first I couldn’t believe he was introducing so many different characters, I felt like I’d walked round a corner in small Spanish town to find the entire population bearing down on me demanding my attention. Then I realised they weren’t all different characters just different names for the same characters and then I got completely lost. So many names, so many ‘Dons’ who also seemed to be priests. I needed a who’s who crib sheet even before the central character had appeared. Then when she did, I took several pages to work out all the names he used for her. There was some great writing but the naming of people was so confusing. I was heart-broken, after looking forward to this so much. I’m terrible with names at the best of times and simply couldn’t go on. I gave up and put the book aside.<br />Has anybody else felt the same? Is it worth persevering/ taking up the book again? Does the range of names settle down? Does anyone have a cheat sheet they could recommend?<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6671621071090419777" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50479751" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14014714440343960947">This book is truly a masterpiece of Realism. However, it is definitely not for the faint of heart or people looking for a light read. The book is not particularly entertaining, and it reads like an 800-page anticlimax. However, it is full of insight into XIX century Spanish society, with each of the characters (whether main or secondary, or even incidental characters)so carefully drawn out that a portrait would be less accurate.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1077584078" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1077584078" />
    <a title="Czarny Pies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies"><img alt="Czarny Pies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400687408p2/29160274.jpg" /></a>

<div id="review_469903652" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/469903652" />
    <a title="Dwight" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1393281-dwight"><img alt="Dwight" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1314134316p2/1393281.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/469903652?book_show_action=true">Dec 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dwight" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dwight" href="/user/show/1393281-dwight">Dwight</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140443462" href="/book/show/765565.La_Regenta">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer469903652" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6502201467994267109"><a href='http://bookcents.blogspot.com/2012/09/la-regenta-by-leopoldo-alas.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>My summary, with links to several posts</a><br/><br/>From one of my posts, Alas the Critic (part 3):<br/> <blockquote>The progressives would present objections, upholding the solidarity of the dogma and the priest, and then Don Pompeyo would have to take sides with the reactionaries, up to a certain point, and say, ‘Let us not confuse one thing with another, gentlemen, the very roots are diseased. The clergy is neither good nor evil, it is as it has to be.’ But on hearing this everybody would oppose him, some for defending th</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText6502201467994267109" style="display:none"><a href="http://bookcents.blogspot.com/2012/09/la-regenta-by-leopoldo-alas.html" rel="nofollow noopener">My summary, with links to several posts</a><br /><br />From one of my posts, Alas the Critic (part 3):<br /> <blockquote>The progressives would present objections, upholding the solidarity of the dogma and the priest, and then Don Pompeyo would have to take sides with the reactionaries, up to a certain point, and say, ‘Let us not confuse one thing with another, gentlemen, the very roots are diseased. The clergy is neither good nor evil, it is as it has to be.’ But on hearing this everybody would oppose him, some for defending the clergy and others for attacking the dogma. He was so right to say that he was utterly alone, that he was unique. From these arguments, which he sought and provoked every day, he affirmed that ‘his spirit—for want of a better word—emerged full of bitterness’ (which was untrue, as his guilty feelings made clear to him), full of bitterness because nobody ever thought in Vetusta, people merely vegetated. Plenty of intrigues, plenty of petty politicking, plenty of material interests (though not even these were properly understood)—but no philosophy, no elevation of the mind to ideal regions. There were one or two scholars, various canonists, a few jurists, but not a single thinker. There was not a thinker in town—apart from himself.<br /> (page 445)<br /><br /> Vetusta the noble was scandalized, horrified. Behind looks of hypocritical compunction the worthy Vetustans concealed from each other the intimate pleasure which that great scandal, just like a novel, gave them, something to interrupt the eternal monotony of the sad city. Very few of them showed that they were pleased about what had happened. It was a scandal! Adultery, discovered! A duel! … A few hours after the catastrophe became known in town, Obdulia Fandiño swept out of her house wearing her largest hat and her most clinging skirt and her most rustling petticoats, to breathe the air of slander, to catch a whiff of scandal, to savour the after-taste of crime being passed from mouth to mouth like a bon-bon which everybody was licking, careful to hide the pleasure afforded by its sticky sweetness.<br /> (pages 706-707)</blockquote><br /><br /><br /><i>La Regenta</i> focuses on the provincial world Alas would have been familiar with in Oviedo. The previous post looked at the importance of literature in Vetustan life and Alas’ use of the arts to develop his characters. Alas spent a lot of time showing the poor cultural aspects of Vetusta, of which literature was only one piece. In this post I’ll touch on another aspect of the culture Alas focused on criticizing that I’ve mentioned several times, that of the desire of the upper classes to copy what they see happening in Madrid and foreign cultural centers in order to soften their small-town ways. The main focus of the post, though will look at the central criticism of the novel—his depiction of the moral corruption in the town.<br /><br />The novel opens with the canon theologian, Fermín de Pas, ascending the cathedral bell tower to survey Vetusta laid out below him, allowing the narrator to provide an overview of the city. As the novel unfolds Alas zooms in on character after character so the reader understands what de Pas was examining. Alas pokes fun at his characters’ provincialism and petty behavior, or rather he lets them demonstrate it. There’s almost too much satire at times, bordering on meanness and losing some of its effectiveness. Fortunately Alas offsets this by providing a rich internal life for his characters so that they are not just caricatures. The Vetustans beclown themselves in many different ways. They imitate what they see and hear in Madrid and Paris. Trying to pass as educated and worldly, their behavior is often rude and boorish. Anyone wanting to be taken seriously in regard to books criticizes authors they have never read. [As an aside, sometimes it’s clear they have read something but completely miss an unspoken point: the archdeacon obviously has Herodotus’ anecdote in mind when he tells “my good Catholic people, ‘inhabitants of the banks of the Nile, those blind heathens whose wisdom impious authors tell us to admire, adored leeks, garlic, onions.’” (page 258)]<br /><br />The frequent mispronunciation and misuse of words (especially Latin phrases) works its way into misquoting literature and mangling history. The Vetustans often demonstrate horribly bad taste, especially in their furnishings, which are often poor imitations of what they claim. They revere attending the theater but ignore what is happening on the stage. At the same time, they allow the theater building to fall into appalling disrepair. The Gentlemen’s Club, the organization and not the building, proves to be an empty shell to facilitate superficial camaraderie—there is nothing of substance inside. Oftentimes their provincialism overlaps with their moral corruption. I provided an extended quote in the previous post on the citizens’ use of Don Santos Barinaga’s declining health to attack the canon while doing nothing to help the ailing man.<br /><br />If not done well Alas, in his skewering of a city rife with moral corruption, could come across as a scold. With the upper classes, almost everything seems to be done for sex. The things reserved for scandal, though, are not the actual trysts but failure to follow the acceptable protocol. Discretion only goes so far—everyone finds out about conquests sooner or later. Those that comment on trysts speak more from envy than from principle. For the upper crust religion is simply another dress to put on when appropriate. Attending services, such as the Christmas Eve mass described, provides a crush of people so that persistent gropes and pinches can occur and enjoyed under acceptable cover.<br /><br />The clergy receives even more wrath from Alas’ pen. Most of the priests are petty tyrants, looking to protect and expand their influence. Their charges are good only for the gossip and secrets they provide. The canon Fermín de Pas turns out to be a very complex character, but the villagers’ charges of simony and vanity are well founded. He turns out to be ruthless in obtaining and wielding power, playing parishioners as pawns for his desires. Alas makes clear that it is the individuals at fault instead of the institution, though, taking pains to show some of the clergy (especially the bishop and Ripamilán) as truly interested in their mission.<br /><br />Vetusta’s provincialism and moral corruption at all levels come together as major components for the central story—the undermining of Ana’s virtue. With the aristocracy so focused on sex, they admire and reward conquests. The city’s Don Juan becomes a role model for the marquis’ son (and this is actively encouraged by his mother). Ana didn’t really stand a chance since so much of the city was aligned against her. Beautiful and sensitive, she never fit in the social circles of her husband. But then she never fit in from an early age. Her governess, frustrated at not seducing Ana’s father, focuses her anger on Ana and believes the worst about her. Ana’s aunts are more practical in their guidance but their marriage pairing leaves a lot to be desired. Many of the citizens watch Ana out of envy, hoping she falls to their level—Vetusta is a town that improves itself by bringing others down. Several of the characters take an active role to assist Mesía (the Don Juan) in his conquest. Some do it out of spite and envy, others to strike at the canon and weaken his influence. Since he was Ana’s confessor, her behavior is seen as an extension of his power. Fermín even sees her this way. One of the more confusing examples of helping undermine Ana’s virtue comes from a religious widow, Doña Petrolina Rianzares. Even though the widow coordinates many of the good works in the city, she also provides the rendezvous site for private meetings between Fermín and Ana—what does she think goes on in her parlor?<br /><br />Small wonder then, given such a perverse environment, that Ana falls. But I’m also interested in the regimen Fermín prescribed to her—it involves doing good works (involving people assisting her pursuer) and reading devotional material (that fails to inspire her long-term). Was his program wrong for Ana, insuring her failure? He recognizes her extraordinary soul, the depth of which has no equal in Vetusta. Yet his thirst for power never ceases and, even though he is one of the few people to appreciate Ana, he helps seal her doom. Among the many contrasts and echoes in the novel, their relationship (echoing the father/daughter feeling with her husband) feels one of the most unnatural. The Good Friday processional in which she participates drives her away from the church and closer to Mesía. At this point it’s only a question of time when Ana’s lack of fulfillment on so many levels will cause her to fall.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6502201467994267109" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1155042887?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Philip Lane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Philip Lane" href="/user/show/4877887-philip-lane">Philip Lane</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1155042887" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2760658670685409998">I found this a bit of a struggle because it is so long - over 700 pages - but I did enjoy it in the main. The main characters are all struggling to one extent or another to control their sexual urges, or at least not allow them to get them into trouble, but of course they do. I thought the three main men were very believable, Don Fermin, a priest who lusts after one of his parishioners, Don Victor, who is unable to satisfy his wife, and Don Álvaro, who is a regular Casanova all appeared to be st</span>
  <span id="freeText2760658670685409998" style="display:none">I found this a bit of a struggle because it is so long - over 700 pages - but I did enjoy it in the main. The main characters are all struggling to one extent or another to control their sexual urges, or at least not allow them to get them into trouble, but of course they do. I thought the three main men were very believable, Don Fermin, a priest who lusts after one of his parishioners, Don Victor, who is unable to satisfy his wife, and Don Álvaro, who is a regular Casanova all appeared to be struggling along well known male lines. I could sympathise with their dilemmas if not really like any of them. The women I found rather less plausible and appeared to have fairly male psyches as they too struggled with their sexuality. La Regenta or Ana goes through a series of phases as she tries to subliminate her physicality and I am not convonced that women have such violent swings but then perhaps a woman reader may have more insight on that than I. It did go on a bit and I felt it would have been better for reducing by at least a third. Good but a bit of a struggle.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_913240273" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/913240273" />
    <a title="Marie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5157049-marie"><img alt="Marie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588700723p2/5157049.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/913240273?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marie" href="/user/show/5157049-marie">Marie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer913240273" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12997115211312630679">This is more of an 1.5 rating, because it had some humorous moments. So, I'll give "Clarín" that. <br/><br/>Why? <br/><br/>Why is this considered such a masterpiece when the characters and its multiple names confused even the same author? Why is it a masterpiece when Madame Bovary said the same, earlier, and with better words? This book goes unsufferably on and on, about how all women can't do anything but give up to the horrible desires of men, how organized religion is bad, how women can have their minds disturbe</span>
  <span id="freeText12997115211312630679" style="display:none">This is more of an 1.5 rating, because it had some humorous moments. So, I'll give "Clarín" that. <br /><br />Why? <br /><br />Why is this considered such a masterpiece when the characters and its multiple names confused even the same author? Why is it a masterpiece when Madame Bovary said the same, earlier, and with better words? This book goes unsufferably on and on, about how all women can't do anything but give up to the horrible desires of men, how organized religion is bad, how women can have their minds disturbed by novels. At unsufferable lengths. <br /><br />I got to the first half, then looked up in Wikipedia to see if I could look up on some Cervantes parallell pointed out by the critics (especially, with El celoso extremeño) as soon as I knew of her marriage with a huge age gap. And because her husband was so absent by a good deal of the novel, so I thought he had died and I missed it, but no. His style can be so painful to read. <br /><br />Being warned by huge red lights about the Madame Bovary parallell, even by the same author, I expected something <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I can't believe this guy keeps you expecting for the adultery to happen, and it happens in the last two chapters for the stupidest reason ever (I mean, no adultery is justifiable, but her only reason is basically because her husband had cheated on her and she wanted revenge)  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> but even though Anna Karenina was extremely long, things happened a bit earlier for you to understand. Turns out that this Anna instead, although she kept the whole book looking at her ideal man out of marriage, waits until <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[she knows her husband has cheated on her, because what else could be as good as to cheat the idiot who cheats on you to justify your sin? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />"Society made me this way", this book screams of her protagonist. I'm tired of women who pity themselves because their only aspiration is to be loved (you know, tragic background excuses her initial very emotional piety which somehow makes room for the grave sins), then they get shamed for being promsicuous (while the truth is that she was if anything emulating St. Therese of Ávila in a game, with her desire to convert Muslims) and then they have bad marriages and the only way to break free is to cheat, but oh well, the poor victim is judged for everyone to see, and the hypocrites that are lustful priests and equally unfaithful nobility judge her!<br /><br />Give me a break. At least Tolstoy didn't want to sell us the idea that Anna Karenina was a poor soul. <br /><br />This sounds like progressive ranting, the city is appropiately named Vetusta which means "Old", "Ancient". And it's all about how "women who don't know better and religious men are so desperate for sex!". How cliché. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12997115211312630679" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6958202437927965412" style="display:none">I'm on a quest of reading as many books as possible encompassing Spanish/Latin American authors by the end of this summer, and this book happened to be on my list. <br />I've actually read it in Spanish since I prefer not to read translated works (loss of meaning), and I was pleasantly surprised by its complexity and overall critique of the social schism in Oviedo. <br />Although I'm not a fan of Clarín, I appreciate his linguistic lyricism and clever innuendos. I think that he’s done a great job at portraying the deceitfulness and corruption of various characters throughout the book. <br /></span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aileen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aileen" href="/user/show/3298044-aileen">Aileen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140443462" href="/book/show/765565.La_Regenta">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3298044-aileen?shelf=1001-books-i-ve-read">1001-books-i-ve-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3298044-aileen?shelf=library">library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1374578465" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17239482333572344531">I wouldn't say this was a riveting read. At over 700 pages, it could easily have lost a couple of hundred of them, but this tale of Dona Ana Ozores, the neglected young wife of a much older ex-judge, torn between taking a lover or giving herself to the church kept me interested. Lots of soul-searching, tears and debilitating illness, it wasn't a happy read. Published in 1885, it actually felt much older to me in comparing how England would have been in that time.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15859477374502061634">FINALLY I'VE FINISHED.<br/><br/>It's not that "La regenta" was all bad. It had its ups and downs. I'm fond of all those ironic moments, ridiculing people's hypocrisy and narrow-mindedness of "the elite", but I feel that the author messed up his main characters. The first part was much better than the second one, when it all got too... religious, and then melodramatic. But overall I'm glad I reached the end.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vxdA+sdjr0ZkshN1+AyTku5TxOo9KjeDOJlhk8K/RHvpEaHIZsOvkzH6Euuj0ylIBD1r37ZcsSwFoHfya1veKg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_1118206'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1118206.Fortunata_and_Jacinta?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Fortunata and Jacinta<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/25574.Benito_P_rez_Gald_s?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Benito Pérez Galdós<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.18 avg rating &mdash; 1,455 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1887\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer7831063280608536371\">Capturing a ninteenth-century Spanish world of political tumult and personal obsession, Benito Pérez Galdós\'s Fortunata and Jacinta tells of two women who love the same man unfailingly—one as his mistress, the other as his wife.\n\nIn this new and comp<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7831063280608536371\" style=\"display:none\">Capturing a ninteenth-century Spanish world of political tumult and personal obsession, Benito Pérez Galdós\'s Fortunata and Jacinta tells of two women who love the same man unfailingly—one as his mistress, the other as his wife.\n\nIn this new and complete translation, Agnes Moncy Gullón presents the detailed realism, the diversity of character and scene that have placed Fortunata and Jacinta alongside the voluminous works of Charles Dickens and Honoré de Balzac. Galdós\'s Madrid, recast from his youthful wanderings through the city\'s slums and cafés, includes the egg sellers and faded bullfighters surrounding Fortunata as well as the quieter, sequestered milieu of Jacinta\'s upbringing. Through Juanito, the lover of both women, the writer reveals Spain as a variegated fabric of delicate traditions and established vices, of shaky politics and rich intrigue. In this vast and colorful world, resonant of Dickens\'s London and Balzac\'s France, Galdós presents his characters with a depth, ambiguity, and humor born of the multiplicity of his scene.\n\nGaldós\'s novels enjoyed, for a time, a wide and attentive readership in Spain. As his reputation grew, however, hostility toward his achievements, envy of his success, and political squabbling hampered his progress, stalling his election to the Royal Academy and, in 1912, thoroughly derailing his nomination as Spain\'s candidate for the Nobel Prize.\n\nThough the political controversies that surrounded Galdós\'s works have long been calmed, this translation by Agnes Moncy Gullón brings alive the tempestuous era in which he lived and wrote, allowing English readers to hear the percussive yet often melodic tones of nineteenth-century Madrid in the correct and casual speech of Jacinta, in the pretty but empty words of Juanito, and in the painfully proper, sometimes vulgar language of Fortunata.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"7831063280608536371\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer wtrSignedOut\' id=\'8_book_1118206\'>\n<div class=\'wtrUp wtrLeft\'>\n<form Cada capítulo consta de un número variable de secuencias de corta extensión, que desarrollan episodios que están mezclados con otros que ocurren simultáneamente. De esta manera el a<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13309978926146615035\" style=\"display:none\">La estructura externa está compuesta de seis capítulos y un epílogo. Cada capítulo consta de un número variable de secuencias de corta extensión, que desarrollan episodios que están mezclados con otros que ocurren simultáneamente. De esta manera el argumento se rompe en multitud de pequeñas anécdotas. Lo importante es la suma de las mismas, que conforma un conjunto de vidas cruzadas, como las celdas de una colmena.\n\nEl marco espacio-temporal es muy preciso: Madrid en unos días del año 1943, en plena posguerra. La historia se basa en un espacio novelesco reducido pero con bastantes personajes que intervienen poco en el transcurso de la obra y dan una visión social coral. De entre los trescientos personajes que aparecen, escasean tanto los de las clases más acomodadas como los de la clase obrera y sectores marginados, predominando la clase media baja, la pequeña burguesía venida a menos, cuyas ilusiones y proyectos de futuro son engañosos y cuyas miradas «jamás descubren horizontes nuevos», en una vida relatada como una «mañana eternamente repetida». La novela aparece como primera parte de una serie que había de llevar el título de «Caminos inciertos».\n\nLa voluntad de reflejar con exactitud la realidad no supone la absoluta neutralidad del autor, que interviene de dos formas contradictorias. En la mayoría de los casos utiliza la técnica objetivista, es decir, se limita a mostrar, a describir desde fuera, sin penetrar en el interior de los personajes. Otras veces, sin embargo, adopta una actitud omnisciente y comenta con ironía las actitudes de los personajes.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13309978926146615035\" href=\"#\" La vida es sueño es, en síntesis, la plasmación barroca de la idea de la fugacidad de la vida con todos los aditamentos geniales de construcción, caracteres y estilo que el autor supo imprimirle. Con este pesimismo radical sobre el valor de la vida humana se interfiere el libre albedrío como afirmación personal de Segismundo —“¿y teniendo yo más vida / tengo menos libertad?”—. Estos dos principios combinados crean una riqueza enorme de sentidos, que en esta edición son desmenuzados críticamente por Ciriaco Morón Arroyo.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"116527893628565324\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer wtrSignedOut\' id=\'24_book_916114\'>\n<div class=\'wtrUp       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5780534.Leopoldo_Alas">About Leopoldo Alas</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/5780534.Leopoldo_Alas"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1395180875p3/5780534.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/5780534.Leopoldo_Alas">Leopoldo Alas
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
54
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11517656176582530702">Leopoldo García-Alas y Ureña (25 April 1852 – 13 June 1901), also known as Clarín, was a Spanish realist novelist born in Zamora. He died in Oviedo.<br/><br/>Alas spent his childhood living in León and Guadalajara, until he moved to Oviedo in 1863. There he studied for the Bachillerato (B.A. degree) and began his law studies. He lived in Madrid from 1871 to 1878, where he began his career as a journalist (a</span>
  <span id="freeText11517656176582530702" style="display:none">Leopoldo García-Alas y Ureña (25 April 1852 – 13 June 1901), also known as Clarín, was a Spanish realist novelist born in Zamora. He died in Oviedo.<br /><br />Alas spent his childhood living in León and Guadalajara, until he moved to Oviedo in 1863. There he studied for the Bachillerato (B.A. degree) and began his law studies. He lived in Madrid from 1871 to 1878, where he began his career as a journalist (adopting the pen-name "Clarín" in 1875) and he graduated with the thesis El Derecho y la Moralidad (Law and Morality) in 1878. He taught in Zaragoza from 1882 to 1883. In 1883 he returned to Oviedo to take up a position as professor of Roman law.<br /><br />Above all, Clarín is the author of <i>La Regenta</i>, his masterpiece and one of the best novels of the 19th century. It is a long work, similar to Flaubert's <i>Madame Bovary</i>, one of its influences. Other influences included Naturalism and Krausism, a philosophical current which promoted the cultural and ethical regeneration of Spain.<br /><br /><i>La Regenta</i> is special for its great wealth of characters and secondary stories, while the main character's description is left slightly unfocused and vague. On the other hand, the downfall of the provincial lady has a place amidst two very diverse suitors: the most handsome man in the city and the cathedral's priest. The depiction of this priest is a key part of the book.<br /><br />For the description of the provincial atmosphere and the city's collective life, Clarín used techniques such as the internal monologue or the free indirect style, which makes the story be narrated by the characters themselves and allows the reader to penetrate in their intimacy.<br /><br />In 1890, he published a new novel, <i>Su único hijo</i>. Even though most critics consider it as a lesser novel in comparison with <i>La Regenta</i>, it is equal to the former in the skill with which the technical resources are used. <i>Su único hijo</i> was originally meant to be the introduction to a trilogy, but aside from an outline and a few fragments of the two sequels, <i>Su único hijo</i> was Clarín's last full-length novel.<br /><br />Apart from these works, Clarín is also the author of magnificent stories and of a large number of journalistic articles. He also wrote an essay, <i>La Literatura en 1881</i> (1882), in collaboration with Armando Palacio Valdés.<br /><br />Leopoldo Alas remains a rather enigmatic figure in the Spanish literary world, leaving a legacy that encouraged the search for God and humanism simultaneously. This aberrant confluence has facilitated the presence of various interpretations regarding the author's writings, most noticeably of his masterpiece, <i>La Regenta</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11517656176582530702" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
